  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_41cc2b43-96d5-4192-afcf-c8a3af16102c'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer607851141216246873">While on holiday, Scott Carey is exposed to a cloud of radioactive spray shortly after he accidentally ingests insecticide. The radioactivity acts as a catalyst for the bug spray, causing his body to shrink at a rate of approximately 1/7 of an inch per day. A few weeks later, Carey can no longer deny the truth: not only is he losing weight, he is also shorter than he was a</span>
  <span id="freeText607851141216246873" style="display:none">While on holiday, Scott Carey is exposed to a cloud of radioactive spray shortly after he accidentally ingests insecticide. The radioactivity acts as a catalyst for the bug spray, causing his body to shrink at a rate of approximately 1/7 of an inch per day. A few weeks later, Carey can no longer deny the truth: not only is he losing weight, he is also shorter than he was and deduces, to his dismay, that his body will continue to shrink.</span>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });
      $('rating_details_tip').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }

<div id="review_23131335" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23131335" />
    <a title="Nancy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/763271-nancy"><img alt="Nancy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435597527p2/763271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23131335?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy" href="/user/show/763271-nancy">Nancy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780765361165" href="/book/show/2350747.The_Incredible_Shrinking_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=shelf-inflicted">shelf-inflicted</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=sf-fantasy">sf-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=i-saw-the-film">i-saw-the-film</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23131335" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5617402462418337364"><b>Posted at <a href='http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2014/11/the-incredible-shrinking-man.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shelf Inflicted</a> </b><br/><br/>After reading about white male privilege, racial oppression, and gender inequality, I found it interesting that I chose to read a book about a man who is losing his height at nearly an inch per week. Not only is he greatly inconvenienced because he can't reach high shelves, he is also losing his power and significance as a man and a human being and reduced to merely survival. It’s an adventure tale, and it has some horror and sci-fi elements. I like how the story didn’t</span>
  <span id="freeText5617402462418337364" style="display:none"><b>Posted at <a href="http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2014/11/the-incredible-shrinking-man.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Shelf Inflicted</a> </b><br /><br />After reading about white male privilege, racial oppression, and gender inequality, I found it interesting that I chose to read a book about a man who is losing his height at nearly an inch per week. Not only is he greatly inconvenienced because he can't reach high shelves, he is also losing his power and significance as a man and a human being and reduced to merely survival. It’s an adventure tale, and it has some horror and sci-fi elements. I like how the story didn’t feel dated, despite being written in the 50’s. Scott Carey was not always a likable character, but he was believable and I felt his anguish over every inch he lost and the changing relationship with his wife and daughter, his encounter with bullies, a child molester, and a predatory black widow spider. The ending is sad and surprisingly hopeful. <br /><br />I enjoyed most of the other stories in this collection. Among my favorites were: <br /><br /><i>The Test</i> - a story that explores how society deals with its aging population and one family’s moral quandary and emotional turmoil over an aging parent who must be tested to determine if he is fit to live. Sad, heartbreaking, and not entirely unrealistic. <br /><br /><i>Mantage</i> was about a writer who, after watching a movie with his wife, wishes he could fast-forward through the drudgery and struggles of his life in order to achieve success faster. <br /><br /><i>Shoofly</i> was about the battle between a harried businessman and the fly that lands in his office. There was so much tension in this story that I wasn’t sure who or what would die at the end. The ending was hilarious!<br /><br />Highly recommended.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5617402462418337364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_157005872" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/157005872" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/157005872?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312856649" href="/book/show/754253.The_Incredible_Shrinking_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=science-horror">science-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=1954-1969">1954-1969</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=horror-classic">horror-classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer157005872" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4642280841171182704">Richard Matheson took the platform afforded him by his tremendous skill as a writer and used it as a platform to confront a serious and important issue that had been kept hidden for too long....<b>MALE SHRINKAGE</b><br/><a href='http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Classic%20TV/?action=view&current=shrinkage-2.jpg' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380347524i/701904.jpg' alt='Photobucket' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>Here was a man that was WAY ahead of his time. Not only was he a gifted writer with an amazing and wide-ranging imagination, but he was also someone who wasn’t afraid to tackle tough and controversial social issues. From general topics like race relations, war and politics to deeply pers</span>
  <span id="freeText4642280841171182704" style="display:none">Richard Matheson took the platform afforded him by his tremendous skill as a writer and used it as a platform to confront a serious and important issue that had been kept hidden for too long....<b>MALE SHRINKAGE</b><br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Classic%20TV/?action=view&amp;current=shrinkage-2.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380347524i/701904.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Here was a man that was WAY ahead of his time. Not only was he a gifted writer with an amazing and wide-ranging imagination, but he was also someone who wasn’t afraid to tackle tough and controversial social issues. From general topics like race relations, war and politics to deeply personal issues like religion, the acceptance of the individual...and of course...male shrinkage. <br><br>It is staggering when you consider that this novel was written back in 1956 during a time when the exact causes and lasting impact of shrinkage were not nearly as well known as they are today. Back in the 1950’s, there was very little research into the physical causes of this phenomenon and almost none on the psychological effects on the male population. It was a “taboo” subject that was just not discussed even between fathers and sons. Some have speculated this lack of communication between the generations on this most important issue may have contributed to the tremendous social upheaval that occurred during the 1960’s and 1970’s. <br><br>Of course, nowadays there is a much better understanding among the scientific community about the phenomenon of male shrinkage as evidenced by the illustration below that was used at a recent medical conference to explain the basic science behind the condition:<br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Decorated%20images/?action=view&amp;current=Dscn0453-1-1.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380347524i/701905._SX540_.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Nevertheless, despite not having the scientific research available and despite the conservative time in which this story was written, Matheson managed to write what is arguably the most in depth and thoughtful discussion of male shrinkage that has ever been written. I can only applaud Mr. Matheson for showing the world that this issue can be addressed in a compassionate, intelligent way. <br><br>Of course, given the social mores of his time, Matheson was forced to disguise the central theme of his story by having his plot revolve around the shrinking of the main character’s ENTIRE body. However, despite this bow to conventionality, the astute reader from the very beginning is able to see through the thinly veiled disguise and into the truth behind Matheson’s powerful story.<br><br>As the story begins, Scott Carey is a man of “above average” size with a wife and daughter. While on the ocean, he is doused with “radioactive” water that causes him to <u>immediately</u> begin shrinking. From there the story is told through two interweaving plot lines. The “present” shows Scott when he is <i>shudder</i> 5/7 of an inch big, hiding in his basement and living a survivors existence trying to find food and water while battling a Black Widow spider and an oversized pussy cat (the obvious symbolic meaning of these two are discussed below). Meanwhile, the second plot thread is a series of “flashbacks” in which we see Scott dwindle from his above average size all the way down to the point where Scott is so small that he no longer even sees himself as a man. <br><br>It is during these flashbacks that I think Matheson’s writing and story-telling is at its most poignant and compelling. Once Carey loses his above average size, his self esteem and rational demeanor begin to erode at once. <i>Does anyone still think Matheson is talking about “height”?....I think not!!</i> As Scott continues to lose inches, he is unable to concentrate at work and becomes obsessed with what he views as kid glove treatment from those who know about “IT.” <br><br>Almost immediately after Scott experiences the first signs of shrinkage, his marriage begins to suffer (big surprise, huh). Scott feels as though his wife is less attracted to him and sees her attempt to maintain a normal relationship as condescending. This deterioration continues as Scott gets smaller and smaller until there is no physical contact at all between Scott and his wife. In the negative reaction of Scott's wife, Matheson again shows how he was decades ahead of his time in recognizing and confronting what in modern times has become a cliche.....<br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Decorated%20images/?action=view&amp;current=motivator3426622-1.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380347524i/701906.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Matheson was brilliant in his depiction of the psychological trauma that accompanied Scott’s reduction in size and I don’t believe there has ever been a more evocative examination of this deeply emotional issue. The fact that all of this <b>deep and penetrating</b> analysis needed to be done behind the veil of an “acceptable” plot makes the depth of the writing even more amazing. <br><br>Here are just a few examples of Matheson’s superb ability to write an acceptable upon surface story while engaging in heated, passionate intercourse upon his true themes: <br><br><u>Surface Plot</u>: As mentioned above, the trauma for Scott begins story begins with him being doused with water after which he immediately begins to experience shrinkage....<i>Subtext</i>: Scott is shown to be the “everyman” as he is confronted with the classic factors contributing to shrinkage. <br><br><u>Surface Plot</u>: Chapter headings are all done in inches <u>alone</u> (e.g., 68”, 64”, etc.) rather then the more common height measurements of feet and inches....<i>Subtext</i>: Matheson’s use of the common unit of male size rather than height is a clear indication that he was asking his readers to strip away normal conventions. <br><br><u>Surface Plot</u>: As Scott finds himself <u>smaller</u> and <u>smaller</u> he is confronted by the main “monster” of the story....a <b>female Black Widow</b> spider.... <i>Subtext</i>: The subtle imagery in this plot device is almost magical in its invocation of the often harsh, uncaring female when it comes to the issue of shrinkage. <br><br><u>Surface Plot</u>: Almost as telling is Scott’s dangerous encounter with an oversized pussy cat just after going through a heartfelt inner monologue about his concern over his current size.....<i>Subtext</i> Here Matheson seems to be touching on the fear and angst that can accompany someone regarding the physical act of love when dealing with excessive shrinkage. The appearance of the “cat” so soon after Scott’s internal expression of inadequacy appears to confirm this. <br><br><u>Surface Plot</u>: A central fixture of the plot when Scott is at his smallest is the “water heater” in the basement which provides both a critical source of sustenance as well as a primary obstacle to overcome......<i>Subtext</i>: Here we have the constant reminder that while cold water is the enemy and cause of shrinkage, warm water can just as quickly bring salvation. <br><br>While there are many more examples of Matheson’s subtle exploration of this provocative issue, the above provides enough to demonstrate the nuance and skill exhibited by Matheson in this ground-breaking novel. I would strongly recommend you read the story yourself so you can see the full range of Matheson's gift. <br><br>It is possible that some out there may be uncomfortable reading this review and addressing the issue of shrinkage in this forum. However, after reading Matheson’s brilliant and courageous story, I have been motivated to <u>expose</u> this issue and hope that through awareness and education, we can create compassion and understanding for this most painful and personal of issues. He has given me the courage to take up his mantle (unworthy as I may be) and shout out from the rooftops.....<b>EXPOSURE TO COLD WATER CAN CAUSE SIGNIFICANT <u>BUT TEMPORARY</u> REDUCTION IN SIZE THAT CAN CAUSE AN OBSERVER TO PERCEIVE <i>DEFICIENCIES</i> THAT ARE NOT TRULY THERE.</b><br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Goodreads/?action=view&amp;current=tinyweiner-1.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380347524i/701907.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="4642280841171182704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer17260819778530073484">It's been a really long time since I last read this & even longer since I've seen the whole movie, but there were some iconic scenes that stayed with me. Rereading it was a great idea since I'd forgotten how masterfully Matheson had woven the story together. The great environmental message about how he comes to shrink through massive, random exposure to radiation & a pesticide.<br/><br/>The most exciting thread is his struggle in the basement as he shrinks from 1" down even further. He's battling hunger, </span>
  <span id="freeText17260819778530073484" style="display:none">It's been a really long time since I last read this &amp; even longer since I've seen the whole movie, but there were some iconic scenes that stayed with me. Rereading it was a great idea since I'd forgotten how masterfully Matheson had woven the story together. The great environmental message about how he comes to shrink through massive, random exposure to radiation &amp; a pesticide.<br /><br />The most exciting thread is his struggle in the basement as he shrinks from 1" down even further. He's battling hunger, cold, &amp; the spider. Worse, he knows he has only days left as he shrinks 1/7" daily. What is a 0" man besides dead? Yet he struggles on to escape from the basement prison.<br /><br />In between these battles &amp; bouts of despair, Carey remembers the earlier part of his shrinking. There he struggled with the loss of his family as his decreasing size alienates them. Best of all, he keeps us in suspense as to how they lost him to the basement &amp; we don't find out until the very end.<br /><br />And then there is the end. Perfect. Truly a SF classic. There were some inevitable inaccuracies in the science aspect, but they were easy to overlook. This version was very well narrated, too. Highly recommended. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17260819778530073484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2631999263" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2631999263" />
    <a title="Lee  (the Book Butcher)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/87153885-lee-the-book-butcher"><img alt="Lee  (the Book Butcher)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605550410p2/87153885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2631999263?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee  (the Book Butcher)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee  (the Book Butcher)" href="/user/show/87153885-lee-the-book-butcher">Lee  (the Book Butcher)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312856649" href="/book/show/754253.The_Incredible_Shrinking_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2631999263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7371088269216322450">a good mixture Psychical and psychological horror. From a OG of modern horror Richard Matheson.<br/>The plot as the title suggests is about a man who is shrinking a little everyday. told in two parts the present features the Psychical. The Psychological is told using flashbacks from the past as Scott Carey tries to come to terms with what is happening to him!<br/><br/>First I want to discuss the more successful psychical horror. Scott's day to day survival is full of challenges and perils. Like black widow spi</span>
  <span id="freeText7371088269216322450" style="display:none">a good mixture Psychical and psychological horror. From a OG of modern horror Richard Matheson.<br />The plot as the title suggests is about a man who is shrinking a little everyday. told in two parts the present features the Psychical. The Psychological is told using flashbacks from the past as Scott Carey tries to come to terms with what is happening to him!<br /><br />First I want to discuss the more successful psychical horror. Scott's day to day survival is full of challenges and perils. Like black widow spiders, the family cat, huge drops, insurmountable cliffs, and the possibility of being stepped on by giant humans who can't see him. The opening scene where Scott is running through a supposed desert from a giant monster Spider is brilliant and probably one of the best opening chapters in the horror genre. You're thrown in not knowing that the desert is just the cellar floor and the spider is just a common black widow. setting the pace for the rest of the book's break neck action in the "present chapters." Scott is also almost likeable in the present parts you root for him. he shows valiant resolve, courage, and ingenuity. You follow him though all his troubles and marvel at his plight. The perspective of someone that small is also a highlight as everything takes on new context. <br /><br />Now to address the less successful psychological horror. These chapter deal with how Scott became the shrinking man (radioactive pesticide, god i love that 50's sci-fi science). And his mental state through the process. there is some good horror here. Like ruminations on what will happen as he shrinks, Will he shrink to nothingness? will he fade away? the book also touches briefly on a real life fear of cost of treatment. Can he afford treatment? Will the cost of treatment make his family destitute after he is gone? Is there any point to trying treatment? As i said a real life modern fear. But Matheson does not expand on those ideas. as you can guess if Scott Carey is almost likeable in the "present" chapters he must be unlikeable in the "flashback" chapters. you would be right Scott is selfish, angry, self-pitying and in short a jerk to his family and those around him. All his emotions are almost justified, believable, and show depth. but Matheson plays a close game here with the reader's sympathies IMO. I'm sure many readers stopped caring about what happens to the jerk. But i held on, Scott's story is his own and although i would like to think i would behave differently i don't know. <br /><br />Scott Carey develops throughout the story. the Scott when he first get diagnosed is completely different than the Scott at the end. this is good writing. to do character development in the kind of flashback format used here. But you have to stick around to the end to appreciated it. That might be the problem and what damage is done along the way. This is why the story lost a star from me. <br />The two types of horror meet at the end Where Scott expects to disappear into nothing. But the ending really surprised me. This is the second Matheson book i have read the other being I Am Legend and both endings were Jaw droppingly spectacular. He might close a book as well as anyone in literary history! <br /><br />Matheson is the master of in depth psychological horror he gets you into a character head then mixes it well with Psychical terror. you can see his influence on Stephan king's work! One final thought and i hesitate to mention it. Matheson seems to only write in the much maligned "male gaze". i mention this because he does not seem to be interested in the female perspective at all. Sign of the times it was written i guess. This was a little jarring to me after many modern reads. but instead of getting upset I'm going to call it progress that modern authors make sure both sexes are represented in modern literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7371088269216322450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17493476552487032623" style="display:none">A Timeless novel, Matheson is such a great writer he writes with many themes undead, ghosts, haunted houses , shrinking man and human endeavors, I only wish he had written more novels, this story is of the highest caliber! <br>This is a story of survival for one man and his emotional and psychological journey as life slips away from beneath his feet Inch by Inch literally. We follow his realization and self-discovery with this fate put before him.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>"He looked at her full body again, feeling breath catch him uncontrollably. It wasn't just physical desire it was so much more. It was the dread of tomorrows without her. It was the horror of his plight, which no words could capture. For it was not a sudden incident removing him from her life. It wasn't a sudden illness taking him, leaving the memory of him intact, cutting him from her love with merciful swiftness. It wasn't even a lingering sickness. At least then he'd be himself and, although she could watch him with pity and terror, at least she would be watching the man she knew. This was worse, far worse."<br><br>"Odd that after all the moments of abject terror he had suffered contemplating the end of his existence, this night - he felt no terror at all. Hours way lay the end of his days. He knew, and still he was glad he was alive. That was the wonderful part of this moment. That was the thick blanket of contentment that warmed his toes. To know the end was close and not to mind. This, he knew, was courage, the truest, ultimate courage, because there was no one here to sympathize or praise him for it. What he felt was felt without the hope of commendation. Before it had been different. He knew that now. Before he had kept on living because he had kept on hoping. That was what kept most men living."<br><br>"But now, in the final hours, even hope had vanished. Yet he could smile. At a point without hope he had found contentment. He k re he had tried and there was nothing to be sorry for. And this was complete victory, because it was a victory over himself."<br><br>"I've fought a good fight, 'he said. It sounded funny to say it. He felt almost embarrassed. Then he shook away embarrassment. It was what was left to him. Why shouldn't he proclaim the bitter sweetness of his pride?<br>He bellowed at the universe. I've fought a good fight! And under his breath he added God damn it to hell. It made him laugh. His laughter was the faintest icy sprinkling of sound against the vast, dark earth. It felt good to sleep, under the stars."</i>

<div id="review_887425992" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/887425992" />
    <a title="Selkie ✦ Queen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28165536-selkie-queen"><img alt="Selkie ✦ Queen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587803822p2/28165536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/887425992?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Selkie ✦ Queen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Selkie ✦ Queen" href="/user/show/28165536-selkie-queen">Selkie ✦ Queen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28165536-selkie-queen?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28165536-selkie-queen?shelf=my-reviews">my-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28165536-selkie-queen?shelf=book-diet-2014-wave-2">book-diet-2014-wave-2</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer887425992" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16830061062468540504">Considering this is a Richard Matheson book, an author who is probably best known for his horror stories, I have initial expectations that this was going to be a scary venture in the same manner as <i>Hell House</i> was when I saw the movie as a child and later on read the book. But in the first fifty pages or so of this novel, my expectations were met in a different way yet it was also something more satisfying which could be what Matheson has intended when he wrote it. <br/><br/><i>The Shrinking Man</i> tells the sto</span>
  <span id="freeText16830061062468540504" style="display:none">Considering this is a Richard Matheson book, an author who is probably best known for his horror stories, I have initial expectations that this was going to be a scary venture in the same manner as <i>Hell House</i> was when I saw the movie as a child and later on read the book. But in the first fifty pages or so of this novel, my expectations were met in a different way yet it was also something more satisfying which could be what Matheson has intended when he wrote it. <br /><br /><i>The Shrinking Man</i> tells the story of Scott Carey who was one day sprayed with a radioactive chemical by accident, and found himself physically shrinking since. The novel perfectly opens with a very terrifying description of Scott being chased down by a spider. At first glance, this book seems to be a very simplistic survivalist story about one man's struggle to endure a hopeless circumstance--but the existential horror that is the overall thematic scope of the plot is definitely its most intriguing aspect. This could almost be an episode in <i>Twilight Zone</i> and that's probably the strength of Matheson's work as a horror writer. <br /><br />In <i>The Shrinking Man</i>, he gives us a chilling glimpse at the visceral terror of physical helplessness. Scott Carey's anxiety is not just about being erased from existence entirely but it's also about the gradual loss of his relevance as a person of flesh and blood. A man who used to be six-foot tall, he now has to deal with the emasculation of his role both as a husband and father. Scott may be shrinking into a size that's even below his kid daughter, but he still has the same needs and entitlement as any grown man does--and the harrowing and pitiful ways he tries to hold onto these things but fail are almost hard to read for me.<br /><br />Matheson needs to be commended for his clear-cut prowess as he delicately approached the writing of this story with such an earnest tone even though it has an absurd premise. What Matheson and the readers end up with is a massively heartfelt tale about the importance of spiritual optimism and the ways that a man can still see a point in living despite the uncertainty and despair he faces. The book can also be seen as a deconstruction of masculine roles in society and what happens when those very rigid notions are inspected and essentially stripped away which is the case with Scott Carey's character as we put ourselves in his position of estrangement from the tangible reality including his family.<br /><br /><i>The Shrinking Man</i> ended with a sincere resolution that is bittersweet and unexpected; Scott Carey has feared about non-existence because he had only defined it in terms of the human context, neglecting the reality that the nature of the universe is not as black-and-white as our own limited perspectives as mortals. Scott Carey, now in his microscopic size, is fortunate enough to witness the everyday miracles of life especially now that he's removed from human bias and that for me is by far the most uplifting kind of pay-off in a science fiction novel that explored such an existential journey. This is a great book filled with engrossing psychological reflections about primal survival instincts and resignation to an inevitable outcome.<br /><br /><b>RECOMMENDED: 8/10</b><br /><i>* A tedious build-up that was able to find its perfect prose rhythm once the protagonist continues to grow and thrive himself in spite of his physical shrinkage.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="16830061062468540504" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_164267691" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/164267691" />
    <a title="Kat  Hooper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper"><img alt="Kat  Hooper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533675060p2/2177891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/164267691?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat  Hooper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat  Hooper" href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper">Kat  Hooper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781433212604" href="/book/show/2350746.The_Incredible_Shrinking_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2177891-kat-hooper?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer164267691" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2585314800557352004">ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a>.<br/><br/>Every day Scott Carey is getting shorter by 1/7 of an inch. The doctors have figured out why -- he was exposed to a combination of insecticide and radioactivity -- but so far they have not been able to make him stop shrinking. Now Scott is only one inch tall and he is trapped in the cellar of his family's rented home with a stale piece of bread, an out-of-reach box of crackers, a sponge, a garden hose, a water heater, and a black widow spider. And in seven</span>
  <span id="freeText2585314800557352004" style="display:none">ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a>.<br /><br />Every day Scott Carey is getting shorter by 1/7 of an inch. The doctors have figured out why -- he was exposed to a combination of insecticide and radioactivity -- but so far they have not been able to make him stop shrinking. Now Scott is only one inch tall and he is trapped in the cellar of his family's rented home with a stale piece of bread, an out-of-reach box of crackers, a sponge, a garden hose, a water heater, and a black widow spider. And in seven more days, he'll be gone.<br /><br />Well, that's enough to make many readers want to hear Scott's story. How did he get in the cellar? Why didn't he prepare for this since he had plenty of time? Where is his wife and daughter? Will the therapies reverse the shrinkage? Will the spider get him?<br /><br />Readers who are expecting a horror-adventure story will be pleased with Richard Matheson's The Incredible Shrinking Man because there's plenty of scary excitement. Spiders, cats, and sparrows are monsters (and so are toddlers); the oil burner is a giant tower with an unpredictable roaring flame; the garden hose is a viper; the sand pile is a desert; the repairman is a giant; pins are spears and a spool of thread is a rope. That story by itself is fun and fascinating.<br /><br />But it's the rest of the story -- the flashbacks, marked with Scott's height as he continues to shrink -- that make The Incredible Shrinking Man such an excellent book. For this story is less about the horror of being physically small than it is about the horror of being physically different and, specifically, about losing manhood. Scott was originally 6'2" and he had a good job and a loving wife and daughter. But as he gradually loses height, he also gradually loses his place as an employee, a husband, a father, and a man. It is this change that is horrifying to watch and made me consider what it means to be a man -- the importance of height, strength, respect, the ability to provide, and even the pitch of the voice. And then a heartbreaking scene at a carnival reminds us that "reality is relative" -- much of how we are perceived (and therefore how we perceive ourselves) depends on our position relative to others.<br /><br />The Incredible Shrinking Man is so much more than an exciting and well-written horror story -- it's a beautiful psychological study of masculinity and loneliness. I listened to Blackstone Audio's version. It's eight hours long and excellently read by Yuri Rasovsky. I highly recommend this version.<br /><br />ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2585314800557352004" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2688504354" />
    <a title="Thomas Strömquist" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32669562-thomas-str-mquist"><img alt="Thomas Strömquist" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590007844p2/32669562.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2688504354?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas Strömquist" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas Strömquist" href="/user/show/32669562-thomas-str-mquist">Thomas Strömquist</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780795315701" href="/book/show/12308503-the-shrinking-man">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2688504354" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10415332262117889152">I wish I could return this book and get the one that most of my friends here read instead. Naw, just kidding, I'm sure I read this all wrong. But even after really gritting my teeth in my efforts to suspend disbelief - or actually turn a blind eye to facts and physics, I really couldn't find much engaging in this story. <br/><br/>Scott shrinks 1/7th of an inch each day (radiation+insecticides effects obviously follow the imperial metric system), which of course works out to exponential shrinking as it goe</span>
  <span id="freeText10415332262117889152" style="display:none">I wish I could return this book and get the one that most of my friends here read instead. Naw, just kidding, I'm sure I read this all wrong. But even after really gritting my teeth in my efforts to suspend disbelief - or actually turn a blind eye to facts and physics, I really couldn't find much engaging in this story. <br /><br />Scott shrinks 1/7th of an inch each day (radiation+insecticides effects obviously follow the imperial metric system), which of course works out to exponential shrinking as it goes from a couple of ‰ per day starting out to 25 - 33 - 50 % and beyond as he edges into sub inch height. Anyway, I said I should disregard reason here, so what about metaphor and symbolism? Yes, there's plenty of course and plenty of analyses of the story available if you are inclined. Unfortunately, for me, I did not find it all terribly interesting or engaging. I feel that the story, content and the characters haven't really aged well. Surprisingly much comes down to the main character's sense of sexual adequacy and parts are quite explicit. The lusting after a 16 year old girl (supposedly because she's smaller than the wife) and the coupling with a midget woman (with the consent of said wife, because it's obviously absolutely necessary) does little to rouse any sympathy for the protagonist. When he himself is exposed to a paedophile, is another matter and there I thought this side of the story kind of worked. <br /><br />OK, let's look at it as purely an 'adventure' type story then, running from the house cat, battling a Black Widow spider, climbing workbenches and the like. Well, let's not actually. A very large part of the story is taken up with descriptions of relative sizes and incredibly boring descriptions of excursions in a normal cellar when you are but an inch or so and I gave up instantly trying to see the scene of sewing needles, thread, stairs, paint jar handles, sponges with Scott traversing, climbing, jumping, balancing... Top the entire thing off with a bit of a cop-out ending (or non-ending if you will) and this one is not a book I'd recommend. The second star is more for the 'classic' status than anything else...</span>
  <a data-text-id="10415332262117889152" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1455713284" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1455713284" />
    <a title="David (דוד) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5431286-david"><img alt="David (דוד) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604688233p2/5431286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1455713284?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David (דוד) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="David (דוד) " href="/user/show/5431286-david">David (דוד) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5431286-david?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5431286-david?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5431286-david?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5431286-david?shelf=new-author-trial">new-author-trial</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5431286-david?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5431286-david?shelf=owned-read">owned-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5431286-david?shelf=american-writing">american-writing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5431286-david?shelf=allegorical">allegorical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1455713284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14500302525187908733">The Shrinking Man is a really good book, in the sense of its horrifying idea, style of narration, its meticulous description whenever required, and its hidden sub-layer. This was Mr. Matheson's first story that I read, being amongst his earliest written works.<br/><br/>The book initiates with a very small chapter, which in short, describes the causes of the protagonist's initiation of shrinkage; and continues while he is 5/7th of an inch tall with the following chapter. The story then interweaves with two</span>
  <span id="freeText14500302525187908733" style="display:none">The Shrinking Man is a really good book, in the sense of its horrifying idea, style of narration, its meticulous description whenever required, and its hidden sub-layer. This was Mr. Matheson's first story that I read, being amongst his earliest written works.<br /><br />The book initiates with a very small chapter, which in short, describes the causes of the protagonist's initiation of shrinkage; and continues while he is 5/7th of an inch tall with the following chapter. The story then interweaves with two timelines: one whilst he is shrinking from 68" onwards; and the other where he reduces in height from 5/7th of an inch to      . His rate of shrinkage is 1/7th of an inch per day. This I think is intentionally put, where dividing an inch into seven parts is considered unconventional.<br /><br />The book employs a third-person narrative during both the interweaving narratives.<br />[Narration I] When Scott is under an inch in height, his narrative describes his mundane activities, but which are the only ones that can be described because his size is so small in relation to the 'large stuff of materials' around him. The distances for him to travel from a place to another are vast considering his scale, and quite a lot can take place within that time. And not to forget, it is also about weight. Personally to me, it took a while to imagine and get accustomed to a perspective from a tiny man, and the descriptive details of his mundane activities got me bored indeed at various times. Though I understand that that <i>has</i> to be what the author can describe for such a person at that scale most of the times.<i>
  <blockquote>     He'd been running since it had all started. Running physically, from the man and the boys and the cat and the bird and the spider, and - a far worse kind of flight - running mentally. Running from life, from his problems and his fears; retreating, backtracking, facing nothing, yielding, giving in, surrendering.<br />     He still lived, but was his living considered, or only an instinctive survival? Yes, he still struggled for food and water, but wasn't that inevitable if he chose to go on living. What he wanted to know was this: Was he a separate, meaningful person; was he an individual? Did he matter? Was it enough just to survive?</blockquote>
</i>[Narration II] While Scott is shrinking from 68" through to becoming as small as being about a foot in height, the narration does not deal with mundane activities. This is the time when the writer describes of what the protagonist has to face during the period of his shrinkage: implications on his social and family life, the problems that he faces with his inability to use his sexual drive and the way he has to cope with it, the loss of authority of fatherhood to his daughter and in turn losing her respect, the insanity of going through a celebrity status, his thoughts on hopes of survivalism, questions on whether human life is valuable, purposeful and meaningful at such a small scale, etc.<i>
  <blockquote>It was a thing about people. They did not look down expecting to see anything but dogs and cats.</blockquote>
</i> Some of these questions also come during the other narrative. This narration was however, the better part of the book for me, personally.<br /><br />The story also has a sub-layer under it's surface, that is allusive to something like the discrimination under a Class System as just one example. The problems that the protagonist faces while he is shrunk are allusive to a Poor Man, a human who is cast in a society as being in a Lower or Working Class or the Poor Class, an outcast even perhaps; someone who can easily lose hope, and might even question the value and use of his presence in existence. Towering against him is a human being of a larger height as being allusive of a Higher Class in the Society, having easy access to all the available resources of life and leading a well-to-do life, relatively.<br /><br />Overall the story is great; the ending even wonderful, especially for those times (during the 1950s to 70s, perhaps even 80s), and even now to many. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The idea of humanity's limited perceptions of scaling across dimensions, and not-to-forget the Zero, is also brought to attention to the reader. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> One's own gradually physical shrinkage sounds extremely horrifying to me after reading the book and living in the world of a shrunken protagonist within the last few days. A very good read indeed, for an SF/Horror classic. Much recommended. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14500302525187908733" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_140014590" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/140014590" />
    <a title="Alazzar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3531937-alazzar"><img alt="Alazzar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328030890p2/3531937.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/140014590?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alazzar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alazzar" href="/user/show/3531937-alazzar">Alazzar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312856649" href="/book/show/754253.The_Incredible_Shrinking_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer140014590" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4938972835725679096">Thank God it’s over.<br/><br/>I wanted to like this book. I really did. From the moment I saw the cover (a tiny man fending off a spider with a spear-sized needle—how awesome is <i>that</i>?), I wanted to read this story by an author I thought could do no wrong.<br/><br/>When I had 5 pages left, I almost stopped reading. Not because I was disappointed with the direction of the plot, but because I <i>just couldn’t take it anymore</i>.<br/><br/><i>The Shrinking Man</i> is the story of Scott Carey, AKA “The Flash” from DC Comics. (I figure he must </span>
  <span id="freeText4938972835725679096" style="display:none">Thank God it’s over.<br /><br />I wanted to like this book. I really did. From the moment I saw the cover (a tiny man fending off a spider with a spear-sized needle—how awesome is <i>that</i>?), I wanted to read this story by an author I thought could do no wrong.<br /><br />When I had 5 pages left, I almost stopped reading. Not because I was disappointed with the direction of the plot, but because I <i>just couldn’t take it anymore</i>.<br /><br /><i>The Shrinking Man</i> is the story of Scott Carey, AKA “The Flash” from DC Comics. (I figure he must be some sort of incarnation of the fleet-footed superhero in the red and yellow spandex, because no matter how small Scott Carey gets, he can <i>always outrun whatever is chasing him</i>. Apparently the shorter your legs are, the faster you move.)<br /><br /><i>Logic.</i><br /><br />I’ve read a few other Matheson stories—the novels <i>I Am Legend</i>, <i>Hell House</i> and <i>Woman</i>, not to mention a few of his well-known stories like “Born of Man and Woman” and “Prey”—and I’ve loved them all. Some more than others, admittedly, but I’ve never walked away regretting the time I spent reading them.<br /><br />That all changed with <i>The Incredibly Boring Man (Who Also Happens to be Shrinking, FYI)</i>. (--Original manuscript title.)<br /><br />Things start out great. Five-star book from the beginning. We see a tiny little man struggling to survive in a world that was not built for his size. Every simple task we take for granted—eating, sleeping, not getting eaten by bear-sized spiders—is an arduous endeavor for him. Early on, Matheson goes into great detail regarding just how much work it takes for Scott to survive.<br /><br />And he continues with that level of detail for the <i>rest of the book</i>.<br /><br />If you want to talk about every single foothold this tiny man has to use in climbing a chair 100x his size, great—that’ll really help the reader realize just how small Scott is. But it quickly becomes TOO DAMNED MUCH. (I’m sorry, but I’m experiencing a level of fury right now that can only be expressed through the magic of CAPS LOCK.) <i>Every</i> scene with Scott as a tiny man in the cellar was about 2-3x as long as it needed to be. I get that we’re supposed to experience his frustration, but you’ve got to draw the line somewhere—at a certain point, I feel like <i>I’m</i> the one having to put up with all these insurmountable obstacles, and guess what? It’s NOT ENJOYABLE.<br /><br />Or maybe this was some sort of Matheson meta-brilliance—Scott Carey feels like there’s no hope and that his world will never get better, and that’s the <i>exact same feeling</i> I got about myself while reading the book. Bra<i>vo</i>, Mr. Matheson.<br /><br />(Just to be clear, I still think Matheson is an amazing writer. But man, was this story ever a stinker.)<br /><br />This book probably would have worked better as a novella. There are just pages upon pages of superfluous information. And I don’t necessarily mean there are scenes that needed to be cut—just that the <i>existing</i> scenes should have been chopped in half (or maybe even thirds).<br /><br />I went into this book thinking it was a classic. I was greatly disappointed. But, in the end, I still have one thing to be thankful for:<br /><br />At least it wasn’t <i>Frankenstein</i>.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4938972835725679096" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2936851074" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2936851074" />
    <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour"><img alt="J.M. Hushour" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442964894p2/17463213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2936851074?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.M. Hushour" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour">J.M. Hushour</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2936851074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5912089392226375471">"Reality was relative."<br/>A surprisingly funny and surprisingly downright disturbing "function" story.<br/>By "function" I mean a kind of story that sounds like it'd be uninteresting but which, if done well, never is. Like another recent read, <i>Memoirs of an Invisible Man</i>, Matheson's nightmare tale centers a lot of its drama and fascination on answering the question, "Okay, what if this really did happen? How would a person cope?"<br/>Trapped in a basement for months, the ever-shrinking man battles hunger and</span>
  <span id="freeText5912089392226375471" style="display:none">"Reality was relative."<br />A surprisingly funny and surprisingly downright disturbing "function" story.<br />By "function" I mean a kind of story that sounds like it'd be uninteresting but which, if done well, never is. Like another recent read, <i>Memoirs of an Invisible Man</i>, Matheson's nightmare tale centers a lot of its drama and fascination on answering the question, "Okay, what if this really did happen? How would a person cope?"<br />Trapped in a basement for months, the ever-shrinking man battles hunger and the black widow determined to eat him. How does he live? Get around? Fight? Much of the meat surrounds these questions, and it is a hoot to read for the sheer ingenuity.<br />The surprising bit of this adventure tale is the other half which is Shrinky's introspective descent into madness. Half the time suicidal, his basement exile is paralleled with flashbacks to his early stages of shrinking and a lot of what happens is counter-intuitive. His loss of masculinity as he descends to doll-size and can't fuck his wife, his frailty, his being assaulted by a child molestor, his virtual reduction to a pint-size man-baby...these are all topics brought to the forefront, so it as much a psychological investigation as functional.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5912089392226375471" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer10929795947100722125"><b>Timeless novel Matheson is such a great writer most of his novels written in the 50s it can outdo many of today's stories. He writes with many themes the undead, ghosts, haunted houses , and human endeavours, I only wish he wrote more novels this story is of the highest calibre! This is a story of survival for one man in this emotional and psychological journey as life slips away from beneath his feet Inch by Inch literally. We follow his realisation and self-discovery with this fate put before </b></span>
  <span id="freeText10929795947100722125" style="display:none"><b>Timeless novel Matheson is such a great writer most of his novels written in the 50s it can outdo many of today's stories. He writes with many themes the undead, ghosts, haunted houses , and human endeavours, I only wish he wrote more novels this story is of the highest calibre! This is a story of survival for one man in this emotional and psychological journey as life slips away from beneath his feet Inch by Inch literally. We follow his realisation and self-discovery with this fate put before him.</b><br /> <blockquote>"He looked at her full body again, feeling breath catch him uncontrollably. It wasn't just physical desire it was so much more. It was the dread of tomorrows without her. It was the horror of his plight, which no words could capture. For it was not a sudden incident removing him from her life. It wasn't a sudden illness taking him, leaving the memory of him intact, cutting him from her love with merciful swiftness. It wasn't even a lingering sickness. At least then he'd be himself and, although she could watch him with pity and terror, at least she would be watching the man she knew. This was worse, far worse."<br /><br />"Odd that after all the moments of abject terror he had suffered contemplating the end of his existence, this night - he felt no terror at all. Hours way lay the end of his days. He knew, and still he was glad he was alive. That was the wonderful part of this moment. That was the thick blanket of contentment that warmed his toes. To know the end was close and not to mind. This, he knew, was courage, the truest, ultimate courage, because there was no one here to sympathise or praise him for it. What he felt was felt without the hope of commendation. Before it had been different. He knew that now. Before he had kept on living because he had kept on hoping. That was what kept most men living.<br />But now, in the final hours, even hope had vanished. Yet he could smile. At a point without hope he had found contentment. He knew he had tried and there was nothing to be sorry for. And this was complete victory, because it was a victory over himself. I've fought a good fight, 'he said. It sounded funny to say it. He felt almost embarrassed. Then he shook away embarrassment. It was what was left to him. Why shouldn't he proclaim the bittersweetness of his pride?<br />He bellowed at the universe. I've fought a good fight! And under his breath he added God damn it to hell. It made him laugh. His laughter was the faintest icy sprinkling of sound against the vast, dark earth. It felt good to sleep, under the stars."</blockquote><br /><br />Great stuff!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10929795947100722125" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_823520943" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/823520943" />
    <a title="Kirk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2050288-kirk"><img alt="Kirk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476811824p2/2050288.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/823520943?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirk" href="/user/show/2050288-kirk">Kirk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780425040218" href="/book/show/845726.The_Shrinking_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2050288-kirk?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer823520943" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4332945990938422312"><br/><b>
  <u>A BELATED TRIBUTE</u>
</b><br/><br/>Richard Matheson was my Ray Bradbury, the gateway drug to science fiction books as a teenager. I started with his short stories, probably his best and most consistent terrain, marked by no frills narrative, emotional honesty and often a twisting of the knife. Not long after I read <i>I Am Legend</i>, which is sort of the Velvet Underground of vampire novels, in that it influenced countless other properties many of which no doubt made more money than the original ever did. It’s a ri</span>
  <span id="freeText4332945990938422312" style="display:none"><br /><b>
  <u>A BELATED TRIBUTE</u>
</b><br /><br />Richard Matheson was my Ray Bradbury, the gateway drug to science fiction books as a teenager. I started with his short stories, probably his best and most consistent terrain, marked by no frills narrative, emotional honesty and often a twisting of the knife. Not long after I read <i>I Am Legend</i>, which is sort of the Velvet Underground of vampire novels, in that it influenced countless other properties many of which no doubt made more money than the original ever did. It’s a ridiculously economical book without an ounce of flab, poignant, haunting, and bitter. It’s one of three books read in those years that I remember most fondly (the others being Theodore Sturgeon’s <i>More Than Human</i> and Fredric Brown’s wonderfully pulpy <i>What Mad Universe</i>). He wrote a gonzo, batshit crazy haunted house novel, <i>Hell House</i>. He wrote many of the best <i>Twilight Zone</i> episodes, he wrote screenplays. He was revered by Stephen King, Harlan Ellison, and many others. He died last year.<br /> <br />He disdained bells and whistles. He saw human nature clearly enough that his work rarely dates, never seems tied to a particular decade or era. His material was readily adaptable, and several enduring genre images came from his mind and pen. The implacable, anonymous truck driver who menaces Dennis Weaver in <i>Duel</i>. The Zuni Fetish Doll that menaces Karen Black in <i>Trilogy of Terror</i>. The furry gremlin who menaces a pre-Starfleet William Shatner in <i>Nightmare at 20,000 Feet</i>. He wrote the scripts for several of the Roger Corman adaptations of Edgar Allen Poe, which managed both to be not remotely faithful to the original stories, yet somehow capture much of the mordant gloom of Poe. He wrote the teleplay for the first TV movie of <i>Kolchak: The Night Stalker</i>, a '70s one-season monster-of-the-week wonder that itself would later be one of the main inspirations for <i>The X Files</i>. One of my favorite anecdotes, from an interview with Matheson, regarded his meeting with Alfred Hitchcock, who was looking for a script writer for his upcoming movie, <i>The Birds</i>. Matheson's idea was for the film to not actually show all that much of the birds.<br /><br />It was a short meeting.<br /><br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>THIS PARTICULAR BOOK</u>
</b><br /><br />I'd owned a copy of <i>The Shrinking Man</i> for years but had somehow never read it. And, well...it's not bad but not one of his best. The central virtue is that Matheson clearly thought through the ramifications of a condition in which a man grows inexorably smaller, week by week, with no end in sight. He pulls no punches in detailing the psychic damage and increasing humiliation of the condition, nor the existential terror. That's not insignificant, but there are problems the novel can't overcome. Perhaps the main one is length. Even at 217 pages it feels padded, and I couldn't help thinking that this could have been a brilliant novella of maybe 60 or 80 pages. As it is, there's endless repetition. Scott Carey spends much of the story trapped in his cellar, maybe an inch tall. And while the scenes of him being menaced by a spider are suitably ghastly and visceral (and wisely, not too many of them), the detailing of just how massive formerly ordinary things are to him now--a refrigerator, lawn chairs, boxes--just goes on and on. Similarly, one starts to tire of what we might call The Incredible Kvetching Man. That's probably unkind, given there's no denying how much Scott's plight truly sucks, but when you begin to feel more sympathy for Scott's poor wife Lou (who truly has the patience of Job) than for the short guy himself, that's maybe not a good sign. When he has a moment of insight that <i>"he was nothing but a horrid midget who screamed and ranted in a funny voice"</i>, one thinks, we know, Scott. There are some nicely drawn incidents--when Scott, appearing to be a young boy, hitches a ride with a melancholy possible pedophile; and in particular, a scene of brief connection, of solace really, with a little person, a woman with a traveling carnival, is beautifully rendered and nuanced. The ending of the novel goes existential in a big way. I'm not sure I bought it, but it is a welcome hopeful note to what is genuinely a dark and grim story.<br /><br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>16 RECOMMENDED SHORT STORIES</u>
</b><br /><br /><i>Being<br />Trespass<br />When Day is Dun<br />Born of Man and Woman<br />Dress of White Silk<br />Shipshape Home<br />The Holiday Man<br />The Distributor<br />Day of Reckoning<br />The Likeness of Julie<br />Crickets<br />Mute<br />Girl of My Dreams<br />A Visit to Santa Claus<br />Prey<br />The Finishing Touches</i><br /><br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>5 INDELIBLE POP CULTURE MARKS</u>
</b><br /> <br />5. <i>The Matheson Zone</i><br /> <br />Matheson wrote I think 15 episodes of <i>The Twilight Zone</i>, and contributed some of its best moments. As someone who returns to and rewatches some episodes every decade or so, one thing I notice is that as deft as creator Rod Serling (who wrote about a third of all the episodes) was with a twist ending, his episodes tend toward the socially conventional, and have an unfortunate tendency toward whimsical dreamer husbands with nagging, shrewish wives (usually because they wanted the husbands to go to their jobs and earn a paycheck, the scolds). But Matheson’s episodes resist becoming dated. He wrote <i>Spur of the Moment</i>, in which a woman marries for love instead of money and it turns out to be the worst decision of her life. Because Matheson knew that sometimes, one can act with the best of intentions and end up screwed anyway. He also wrote my personal favorite episode, <i>The Invaders</i>, in which Agnes Moorehead (wthout a line of dialogue) is a solitary woman in a farmhouse menaced by tiny invaders from space.<br /> <br /><br />4. <i>The Baddest Haunted House Ever</i><br /> <br /><i>Hell House</i> is quite bonkers, mostly in a good way, though it arguably flirts with going off the rails toward the end. At any rate, for my money it wipes the floor with <i>The Haunting of Hill House</i>, and I say that as someone who loves and reveres Shirley Jackson. In 1973 it was adapted into the best haunted house film ever, <i>The Legend of Hell House</i>, creepy, scary, violent, with a couple tremendous performances by Pamela Franklin and Roddy McDowell. Do see it, but know that Emeric Balasco isn’t fucking around.<br /> <br /> <br />3. <i>The Last Omega Man on Earth? Not Exactly Legend</i><br /> <br /><i>I Am Legend</i> has been adapted to film three times. None of these quite got it right, though all have their merits. <i>The Last Man on Earth (1964)</i> is an Italian ultra-low-budget effort. Mostly faithful, appropriately bleak tone and cinematography, it’s hampered by the miscasting of Vincent Price as the lead. Robert Neville should be an everyman, and while Price was good at many things, an everyman he was not. (Really, shouldn’t the mutants be more afraid of him?) Also, cinematography aside, the filmmaking here is barely competent, and that’s being generous. <br /><br /><i>The Omega Man (1971)</i>, oh dear. Shall we say it’s not timeless? Shall we say the humans who team up with Neville appear to have just come from a Black Panther rally? On the other hand, Charlton Heston is well cast in the lead. This was during his Angry-Iconoclast-in-Dystopia period. As such, it’s better than <i>Soylent Green</i>, nowhere near as good as <i>Planet of the Apes</i>. And Anthony Zerbe is just great playing Mathias, leader of the mutants (though, don’t bother trying to find this character in the book). Besides, how many movies feature Heston quoting from <i>Woodstock</i> and having a love scene with Rosalind Cash? I leave it to others whether this is a good thing. Dated, cheesy, but never boring, it may be crap, but at least it’s crap on a motorcycle. And it nails the ending.<br /> <br /><i>I Am Legend (2007)</i>, in terms of filmic competence, is leagues above the others. Will Smith does just fine playing Neville. And I liked the liberty they took in giving him a German Shepherd companion. The story is mostly faithful in spirit, if not the details. But a couple major drawbacks. The film’s conception of the mutants is blatantly derivitive of <i>The Descent</i> and <i>28 Days Later</i>. And it completely, COMPLETELY botches the ending.<br /> <br />I’d rate the first two a 6 out of 10, the most recent a 7.<br /> <br /> <br />2. <i>Matheson x Karen Black x 3</i><br /> <br />Ah, <i>Trilogy of Terror</i>. Does anyone who doesn’t remember the ‘70s even know of this? (Note—Netflix has it.) So this cult favorite was a TV movie anthology from 1975, adapting three Matheson short stories (<i>The Likeness of Julie; Therese; Prey</i>), all starring Karen Black. All are effective, but people only talk about the last one, in which Ms. Black is terrorized in her apartment by a Zuni Fetish Doll. It’s insane, hilarious, and terrifying. Sadly, Karen Black also died in 2013. She and Matheson added ‘Zuni Fetish Doll’ to the cultural lexicon, and this is no small thing.<br /> <br /> <br />1. <i>The Truck</i><br /> <br />There are people who will cite <i>Duel</i>, without irony, as their favorite Steven Spielberg movie. Certainly it has to be in the top three. The story is simplicity itself, Dennis Weaver, in the course of a long drive, somehow pisses off the wrong truck driver, who then terrorizes him for miles and miles, implacable, mysterious (you never see his face). Might sound routine, but brilliant in the execution. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4332945990938422312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
